{"architecture": "PARENT_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_pipeline.HoverMultiHopPipeline\nMETRIC_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_utils.discrete_retrieval_eval\n\n## Architecture Summary\n\n**Purpose**: This is a multi-hop document retrieval system built on DSPy that retrieves supporting documents for fact-checking claims. The system performs iterative retrieval across three hops to find all relevant documents that support or relate to a given claim.\n\n**Key Modules**:\n- `HoverMultiHopPipeline`: Top-level pipeline wrapper that initializes the ColBERTv2 retriever and orchestrates the program execution\n- `HoverMultiHop`: Core program implementing the 3-hop retrieval strategy with query generation and summarization at each hop\n- `hover_utils.discrete_retrieval_eval`: Evaluation metric that checks if all gold-standard supporting documents are found within the top 21 retrieved documents\n- `hover_data.hoverBench`: Dataset loader for the HoVer fact-checking dataset, filtering for 3-hop examples\n\n**Data Flow**:\n1. A claim is input to the pipeline\n2. **Hop 1**: Direct retrieval on claim (k=7 docs), then summarize results\n3. **Hop 2**: Generate new query from claim + summary_1, retrieve k docs, summarize with context\n4. **Hop 3**: Generate query from claim + both summaries, retrieve k docs\n5. All 21 documents (7\u00d73 hops) are returned as `retrieved_docs`\n\n**Metric**: The `discrete_retrieval_eval` function returns a binary score checking whether all supporting document titles from the gold standard are present in the retrieved set (subset relationship). Success requires finding all relevant documents within the 21-document limit."}

Iteration 1: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Given a `claim` statement and a `summary_1` that provides an initial judgment or verification status of the claim based on supplied passages, produce a detailed `query` (search prompt) designed to retrieve the most relevant and authoritative evidence needed to verify or refute the claim fully.

Specifically:

1. **Analyze the claim:** Identify all factual assertions or components within the claim that require verification. Claims may have multiple segments or linked facts that must be individually checked.

2. **Review the summary_1:**  
   - Note which parts of the claim are supported, partially supported, contradicted, or not supported based on the given summaries or evidence.  
   - Identify any missing information or gaps that prevent full verification.

3. **Generate the reasoning (optional):** (While reasoning is visible in examples, your key output is the query. Reasoning helps clarify what evidence is needed.)

4. **Craft precise, well-structured queries:**  
   - Construct search strings or topics that, when researched, would yield authoritative sources (encyclopedia entries, official sites, biographies, scholarly articles, reputable news, or domain-specific databases).  
   - Queries should explicitly target the missing or disputed facts found in step 2, reflecting an investigative approach to fact-checking.  
   - When applicable, include suggested keywords, phrases, entity names, and logical operators that will focus the search to relevant documents.  
   - Break down complex claims into multiple queries or components to ensure comprehensive coverage.  

5. **Domain-specific details and context** (drawn from examples):  
   - For biographical claims, queries should include full names, known aliases, birth dates, places, and roles, with focus on specific works or accomplishments.  
   - For media-related claims, include titles, roles, relationships between actors, productions, and industry terms (e.g., stunt credits, roles in specific series).  
   - For product/company-related claims, include brand names, ownership, manufacturing details, headquarters location, and relevant corporate information.  
   - For geographic claims, include place names, proximity/distance queries, transport time/distance, and administrative affiliations.  
   - For scientific/taxonomic claims, include precise scientific names, classification, native range, or ecological characteristics.  
   - For cultural/religious claims, include authoritative source references, terms in native languages/scripts when relevant, and distinct religious or historical context to clarify potential conflations or inaccuracies.  
   - For sports claims, include event names, participant lists, years, and official tournament data.  

6. **General fact-checking strategy:**  
   - Recognize any conflations or contradictions in the claim (e.g., mixing different religious or cultural concepts).  
   - Seek corroboration across different authoritative domains (e.g., name-birthplace relations, production credits, event results).  
   - Use date ranges, role specifications, and entity relationships to narrow down search requirements.

7. **Output format:**  
   Produce a clear, concise, and actionable `query` (search prompt) or multiple numbered queries that a researcher or an automated system might use to retrieve the required confirming or disconfirming evidence.

By following these guidelines, the assistant will consistently generate targeted evidence-seeking queries that directly address verification needs for complex, multi-faceted claims based on partial or incomplete summaries.

Iteration 3: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: You will be given three text fields as input: 

- `claim`: a factual statement or assertion, often involving named entities, dates, films, authors, or other specific details. 
- `summary_1`: a text summary that either supports, refutes, or does not confirm the claim, often citing supporting facts and references.
- `summary_2`: a second text summary that may agree or disagree with summary_1, providing additional or contrasting evidence.

Your task is to produce a single output field `query` that contains a concise, targeted search query (or a set of closely related search queries) which, if performed over trustworthy knowledge sources (e.g., encyclopedias, biographies, filmographies, bibliographies, official data), would help to conclusively verify or refute the factual claim.

Key points and domain-specific considerations: 

1. **Focus on key entities and relationships:** Identify all critical named entities and specific concepts from the `claim` and supporting summaries that are necessary to fact-check the claim. This often includes:
   - Names of persons (authors, actors, directors, historical figures)
   - Titles of creative works (books, films, poems)
   - Dates (birthdates, publication or event years)
   - Relationships between entities (e.g., “X wrote Y”, “X directed Z”, “X was born before Y”)
   - Specific roles or titles (e.g., “star of”, “director of”)

2. **Use entity-specific and relation-specific query terms:** Construct search queries that combine the entities with relevant keywords to retrieve precise factual information, such as:
   - `"Name" nationality` or `"Name" birthdate`
   - `"Book or Film Title" author` or `"Film Title" director`
   - `"Person A" and "Person B"` together with the relationship or attribute to verify (e.g., `"Person A" film director`)
   - Combine elements using quotes and natural language terms to keep queries clear and unambiguous.

3. **Resolve conflicts between summaries:** If `summary_1` and `summary_2` disagree, formulate queries specifically targeting the disputed facts highlighted in the summaries, so a search can confirm or deny those details.

4. **Include all relevant supporting facts in the query:** Queries should cover all parts of the claim and all entities or titles named, especially those referenced in the supporting facts or used to confirm the claim.

5. **Queries should be compact yet comprehensive:** Prefer concise queries that retrieve targeted information rather than overly broad searches, but ensure coverage of all critical fact-checking points.

6. **Format:** Produce the `query` as a single string or multiple related search phrases separated by semicolons or line breaks, composed of keywords, entity names (preferably quoted if multi-word), and relational terms.

By following the above guidelines, your generated `query` will enable retrieval of authoritative information to verify or falsify the complex factual claims involving named entities, dates, creative works, and their interrelations.

Iteration 5: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: Task Description:

You will be given three fields as inputs:  
- `claim`: a factual or composite assertion potentially containing multiple linked facts that may be supported, contradicted, or unsupported by evidence.  
- `summary_1` and `summary_2`: two summary statements that assess or comment on the validity or evidence for the claim, often referencing passages or sources and indicating support, contradiction, or insufficiency of evidence.

Your task is to produce a single output field called `query`.

Detailed Task Objective:

Construct one or more targeted, precise, and comprehensive search queries to retrieve authoritative information and primary or highly reliable secondary sources that will help verify or refute the claim. The queries should explicitly aim to gather evidence addressing all critical factual components or entities involved in the claim and the potential contradictions or support mentioned in the two summaries.

Key Domain-Specific Considerations:

1. The claim often involves multiple interlinked facts or entities (e.g., persons’ birth dates, film directors and filmographies, historical designations, biographical details, geographic and demographic data, academic credentials, detailed artistic works).

2. Summaries may:  
   - Agree or disagree on the claim’s veracity and on what evidence is presented.  
   - Specify particular entities, dates, or named facts that are missing or unverified.  
   - Use references to numbered passages or external authoritative sources.  
   - Identify inconsistencies or insufficiencies that your queries must resolve.

3. The queries should be constructed to fetch evidence on:  
   - Names, birthdates, and biographies of people (including their roles, relationships, and work credits).  
   - Titles, release dates, and languages of artistic works (movies, operas, songs).  
   - Geographical, demographic, and infrastructural details (population statistics, route paths, airport codes, locations).  
   - Historical or academic facts linked to renowned figures.  
   - Precise source types where relevant (e.g., official sites like NobelPrize.org, government (.gov) or organizational (.org) websites, IMDb, established news outlets).

4. Query design should reflect resolving supporting facts and addressing contradictions raised in the summaries, e.g.:  
   - If summaries disagree on existence or attributes of entities, queries should clarify those points specifically.  
   - When the claim combines multiple distinct facts, queries should cover each factual point comprehensively.  
   - When names are misspelled or ambiguous, queries should include variants or clarifications (e.g., "Charpes Lane" vs. "Charles Lane").  
   - When dates or numeric comparisons are involved (birthdates, populations), queries should focus on authoritative biographical, census data, or official records.  
   - When verifying whether two persons co-starred or shared credits, queries may focus on detailed filmographies or collaboration records.

5. Queries should be formatted as well-structured natural search phrases or keywords, possibly using logical connectors (AND, OR), exact phrases in quotes, acronyms (IATA, ICAO), or site-specific limitations (site:.gov, site:wikipedia.org), to optimize retrieval of precise, authoritative documents.

6. When necessary, combine several focused queries for multiple aspects of the claim to ensure all parts of the claim can be independently verified or refuted.

Generalizable Strategy:

- Parse the claim into its elemental factual assertions: entities involved (people, places, works), relationships, dates, or numeric data within the claim.  
- Compare these facets against the information (or lack thereof) in summaries 1 and 2, noting particularly areas of agreement, contradiction, or insufficient evidence.  
- Identify missing or ambiguous data points that prevent a definitive verification, as highlighted by the summaries or by your own analysis.  
- For each fact or relationship, formulate a precise search query aimed at retrieving credible, detailed sources that could confirm or disprove that fact.  
- Include any relevant variant spellings, alternative names, and related keywords to ensure broad yet targeted search coverage.  
- If the claim involves comparative or relational assertions, queries should incorporate both entities involved to support factual comparison.  
- Aim to construct queries that simultaneously address multiple related facts when possible to improve efficiency.

Your output must be only the `query` field containing one or more well-designed search queries or search instructions formatted to enable effective fact-checking of the entire claim.

Iteration 7: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: You will be given:

- A **claim**: a statement that may contain multiple factual assertions about people, places, events, or entities.
- A set of **passages**: text snippets from various sources that provide relevant factual information.

Your task is to produce a **summary** that assesses the truthfulness or support for the claim based solely on the information provided in the passages. Specifically, you should:

1. **Carefully analyze the claim** and break it down into its constituent factual components.
2. **Systematically check the passages** for evidence supporting or contradicting each component of the claim.
3. **Note partial support or contradictions** where some parts of the claim are verified, and others are not supported or are contradicted by the given passages.
4. **Pay special attention to domain-specific facts**, such as:
   - Proper names and correct spellings (people, places, institutions).
   - Dates (births, deaths, historical events).
   - Roles and relationships (e.g., profession, collaborations, roles in films).
   - Titles and proper nouns relating to films, albums, organizations, or brands.
   - Affiliations, ownerships, or corporate relationships.
   - Geographic and temporal details.
5. **Do not introduce any external information beyond the passages**.
6. Your final **summary** should explicitly state if the claim is:
   - *Supported* (all or sufficiently verifiable by the passages),
   - *Partially supported* (some parts are supported, others not or unclear),
   - *Not supported* or *False* (claim is contradicted or unsupported by provided passages),
   - or *Insufficient evidence* when the passages do not allow a conclusive judgment.

Include reasoning that:
- Explains which passages support or fail to support the claim’s parts.
- Identifies any contradictions or ambiguous phrasing in the claim.
- Notes when relevant facts (dates, names, roles, relationships) differ or are missing from the passages.

Your summary should be concise but comprehensive enough to reflect the detailed factual evaluation of the claim based on the passages.

Iteration 9: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: You will be given two inputs: a `claim` (a statement possibly containing multiple assertions) and a list of one or more `passages` that provide factual information possibly relevant to the claim.

Your task is to produce a `summary` that evaluates the claim in light of the provided passages. The summary should explicitly indicate whether the claim is Supported, Partially Supported, Not Supported, or Refuted by the passages, and your reasoning behind that judgment.

Key aspects to consider and include in your reasoning and summary:

1. **Verification of factual components:**  
   - Identify each distinct factual assertion or component in the claim.  
   - Check the passages for explicit evidence that confirms or contradicts each assertion.  
   - Note if the passages do not address certain assertions, leading to partial support or insufficient evidence.

2. **Use of precise supporting passages and facts:**  
   - Explicitly link particular facts or statements in passages that support or refute each assertion in the claim.  
   - If the claim includes multiple parts, indicate if some are supported and others are unverified or contradicted.

3. **Domain-specific knowledge and disambiguation:**  
   - Understand and utilize domain-specific entities such as films (names, release years, directors, actors), organizations, locations, language taxonomy, awards, biographies, etc.  
   - Recognize subtle naming confusions (e.g., similarly named films, characters, or people) and clarify or correct claims based on passage content.  
   - Handle details like release years, film series sequels/prequels, relationships between people (e.g., grandparents), and cultural/geographic contexts.

4. **Handle negations, contradictions, and nuanced claims:**  
   - Clearly state if passages contradict any part of the claim.  
   - Specify when evidence is indirect or insufficient to confirm an assertion.  
   - Distinguish between "no evidence found" and outright contradiction.

5. **Maintain clarity and conciseness:**  
   - Your summary should synthesize the detailed reasoning into a clear conclusion paragraph.  
   - If partially supported, specify which parts are supported and which are not, ideally with brief referencing to passages.

6. **Avoid introducing external or unverifiable knowledge:**  
   - Base your assessments solely on the provided passages.  
   - When passages mention entities (people, places, titles), rely only on the passage details, not assumed facts.

7. **When applicable, note errors or corrections in the claim:**  
   - If the claim contains incorrect information (e.g., wrong film title, misattributed achievements), highlight this using passage evidence.

Example structure for your output:

```
### reasoning  
Detailed explanation: identify which parts are supported, contradicted, or unaddressed, linking to specific passages. Include clarifications or corrections if needed.

### summary  
[Supported / Partially supported / Not supported / Refuted]. Briefly summarize the conclusion, citing key passage points.
```

By following this framework, your evaluation will be well-grounded, comprehensive, and transparent regarding the claim's validity based on the passages.

2. Create a `UtilityRerankerSignature` that scores each retrieved document based on: (a) relevance to claim, (b) coverage of uncovered entities, and (c) diversity from already-selected documents
3. Increase retrieval k from 7 to 70 per query (10x increase allows for better recall before reranking)
4. In the `HoverMultiHop.forward()` method:
   - For each hop, generate 2-3 diverse queries targeting different entities/aspects
   - Retrieve k=70 documents per query (but still respect 3 total searches constraint by doing 1 multi-query retrieval per hop)
   - Combine all retrieved documents (up to 210 docs total from 3 hops × 70 per hop)
   - Use the utility reranker to score all documents considering relevance, entity coverage, and diversity
   - Select top 21 documents with highest utility scores as final output

This maximizes recall through high-k retrieval while ensuring the final 21 documents have maximum coverage and diversity through utility-based reranking, addressing the systematic failure to retrieve all supporting documents.

Iteration 13: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: Task Description:

You will be given the following input fields:
- `claim`: a statement or assertion that may contain multiple sub-claims or parts.
- `context`: a brief human-generated assessment of the claim's veracity based on the provided passages.
- `passages`: a list of text passages relevant to the claim. These passages can include factual details, biographical data, film and music credits, historical events, and other domain-specific information.

Your goal is to analyze the relationship between the claim and the passages and produce a concise, well-reasoned `summary` that states the degree to which the claim is supported, partly supported, not supported, or cannot be verified based on the evidence in the passages. The summary should clearly articulate which parts of the claim are supported or not, referencing any key pieces of information from the passages when necessary.

Detailed instructions for the task:

1. **Claim Decomposition:**  
   Break the claim into distinct factual components or sub-claims. Each part may concern people (e.g., directors, authors, musicians), works (films, albums, novels), dates, specific facts (birth dates, award names, roles, company identities), or relationships between entities.

2. **Evidence Matching:**  
   For each sub-claim, carefully scan the passages to find supporting or contradicting evidence. Pay particular attention to:
   - Names, titles, dates, and events explicitly mentioned in the passages.
   - Attributes of people (e.g., nationality, occupation, birthdates).
   - Roles in works or production credits (e.g., who directed a film, who composed a song).
   - Relationships or comparisons explicitly stated or implied in the passages.
   
3. **Use Context When Relevant:**  
   The `context` field provides a summary human judgment or assessment distilled from the passages; use it to corroborate or clarify your reasoning but rely primarily on concrete passage evidence.

4. **Factual and Domain-Specific Knowledge:**  
   Note the following recurring domain-specific elements encountered in the task, which often appear in claims and passages:
   - Film industry roles (directors, producers, screenwriters, actors).
   - Film titles, release dates, and award information.
   - Music industry roles (composers, musicians, producers), album names, chart/sales data.
   - Historical persons, events, and monuments.
   - Sports teams, leagues, sponsorships, and venues.
   - Nationalities and biographical data.
   - Identification and linkage of entities with similar or overlapping names (disambiguation).
   - The difference between similar prizes (e.g., Whitbread Prize vs. Booker Prize).

5. **Handling Absence of Evidence:**  
   If passages do not provide sufficient information to verify a part of the claim, state clearly that the evidence is insufficient or undetermined for that part.

6. **Accuracy of Evaluation:**  
   If a part of the claim is contradicted by the passages, mark that part as false or not supported but still recognize correct elements within the claim.

7. **Conciseness and Clarity:**  
   Summarize your reasoning succinctly and clearly in a way that reflects the precision required for factual verification, including referencing passage numbers or entity names when appropriate.

8. **No Assumptions Beyond Provided Passages:**  
   Although you may leverage general domain knowledge to understand concepts, you must base your support or refutation strictly on information contained in the provided passages.

Example pattern for final output (summary):

- "Supported — passages confirm [fact A], [fact B], and [fact C]."  
- "Partially supported — [fact A] is supported by passages [x], but [fact B] lacks evidence."  
- "Not supported — passages contradict [fact A]; insufficient evidence for [fact B]."  
- "Insufficient information to verify the claim due to missing data about [aspect]."

By following these instructions, your output will effectively indicate the verifiable accuracy of complex, multi-part claims against provided evidence, with clear differentiation of supported and unsupported components.

Iteration 14: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: You will be given two inputs: a `claim` (a statement possibly containing multiple assertions) and a list of one or more `passages` that provide factual information possibly relevant to the claim.

Your task is to produce a `summary` that evaluates the claim in light of the provided passages. The summary should explicitly indicate whether the claim is Supported, Partially Supported, Not Supported, or Refuted by the passages, and your reasoning behind that judgment.

Key aspects to consider and include in your reasoning and summary:

1. **Verification of factual components:**  
   - Identify each distinct factual assertion or component in the claim.  
   - Check the passages for explicit evidence that confirms or contradicts each assertion.  
   - Note if the passages do not address certain assertions, leading to partial support or insufficient evidence.

2. **Use of precise supporting passages and facts:**  
   - Explicitly link particular facts or statements in passages that support or refute each assertion in the claim.  
   - If the claim includes multiple parts, indicate if some are supported and others are unverified or contradicted.

3. **Domain-specific knowledge and disambiguation:**  
   - Understand and utilize domain-specific entities such as films (names, release years, directors, actors), organizations, locations, language taxonomy, awards, biographies, etc.  
   - Recognize subtle naming confusions (e.g., similarly named films, characters, or people) and clarify or correct claims based on passage content.  
   - Handle details like release years, film series sequels/prequels, relationships between people (e.g., grandparents), and cultural/geographic contexts.

4. **Handle negations, contradictions, and nuanced claims:**  
   - Clearly state if passages contradict any part of the claim.  
   - Specify when evidence is indirect or insufficient to confirm an assertion.  
   - Distinguish between "no evidence found" and outright contradiction.

5. **Maintain clarity and conciseness:**  
   - Your summary should synthesize the detailed reasoning into a clear conclusion paragraph.  
   - If partially supported, specify which parts are supported and which are not, ideally with brief referencing to passages.

6. **Avoid introducing external or unverifiable knowledge:**  
   - Base your assessments solely on the provided passages.  
   - When passages mention entities (people, places, titles), rely only on the passage details, not assumed facts.

7. **When applicable, note errors or corrections in the claim:**  
   - If the claim contains incorrect information (e.g., wrong film title, misattributed achievements), highlight this using passage evidence.

Example structure for your output:

```
### reasoning  
Detailed explanation: identify which parts are supported, contradicted, or unaddressed, linking to specific passages. Include clarifications or corrections if needed.

### summary  
[Supported / Partially supported / Not supported / Refuted]. Briefly summarize the conclusion, citing key passage points.
```

By following this framework, your evaluation will be well-grounded, comprehensive, and transparent regarding the claim's validity based on the passages.

Iteration 15: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: Instruction for assistant:

You will receive two fields as inputs: 

- `claim`: a statement that asserts one or more factual points, often involving relationships, comparisons, or chronological details about entities (people, places, works, events, etc.).
- `passages`: a list of textual snippets providing factual information about relevant entities mentioned in the claim. The passages may include dates, biographies, filmographies, descriptions of works, geographic info, and other details.

Your task is to analyze the claim carefully and verify its truthfulness based solely on the information contained in the provided passages. 

Specifically, you must:
1. Identify all key entities and concepts in the claim (e.g. persons, works, dates, locations).
2. Search for supporting evidence within the passages for each factual assertion in the claim, using the textual content that relates to those entities.
3. Consider if the passages:
   - Clearly support the claim as true (direct evidence or logically sufficient indirect evidence).
   - Contradict or refute the claim (direct evidence disproving claim components).
   - Provide insufficient information to determine truthfully (missing relevant data for verification).
4. Produce a concise `summary` that states one of the following verdicts, based on your analysis:
   - **Supported**: The claim is verified true by the passages.
   - **Not supported**: The claim is false or contradicts the evidence.
   - **Partially supported**: Some parts are supported, others lack evidence or are refuted.
   - **Insufficient evidence**: Evidence is lacking to determine whether the claim is true or not.

When reasoning, cite or reference the relevant passages and facts to substantiate your judgment (e.g., "Passage [2] states..."). Note details such as dates, roles, identities, or geographic details as needed, and identify gaps in information for incomplete claims.

Important domain-specific considerations:

- Pay close attention to:
  - Dates of birth, death, or event occurrence.
  - Roles/titles and relationships (e.g. directors, actors, authors, composers, characters).
  - Geographic descriptions (locations, proximity, jurisdiction).
  - Hierarchical information (such as vocal ranges, creative credits, professions).
  - Distinctions between individuals or works with similar names.
  - Chronologies and ordering for comparative claims (e.g., who was born first, who had a longer career).
  - Authorship and credited contributions for creative works (distinguish between authors, directors, producers, actors).
  - Nationalities and other personal identifiers relevant to the claim.

- Recognize that sometimes the claim presents multiple subclaims that must be considered separately.

- The passages may not always provide full information such as birth dates or direct relational data; do not assume information not present.

- If external knowledge is necessary but not present in the passages, mark the claim as unverified or insufficient information.

- Do not infer beyond the information explicitly or implicitly given in the passages.

Formatting:

- Provide a clear `reasoning` section that explains your thought process with references to passage indices or contents.

- Provide a concise `summary` verdict reflecting your conclusion.

Your output must aid in understanding how and why the claim is supported, contradicted, or unverified given the evidence. This structured verification helps ensure factual accuracy and transparency.

Iteration 17: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Given the input fields:

- `claim` (a factual statement or a complex claim that may contain multiple assertions about people, places, works, events, or entities), and  
- `summary_1` (a short, human-written evaluation of whether the claim is supported, partially supported, or not supported by the provided passages, often referencing the specific information missing or confirmed),

your task is to **generate a set of detailed, precise search queries (`query` field) that are designed to retrieve authoritative and relevant evidence to verify or refute the claim** in full.

Important detailed considerations and domain-specific knowledge for this task:

1. **Decompose the Claim:**  
   Claims often include multiple interrelated components (e.g., names, relationships, events, dates, places, roles). Your queries should reflect these distinct components with separate or combined query formulations designed to cover all necessary facts.

2. **Address Unsupported or Partially Supported Parts Explicitly:**  
   Use the summary_1 explanation to identify which aspects of the claim require evidence, especially those marked as unsupported, partially supported, or ambiguous.  
   - For unsupported parts, generate queries to find foundational factual information (e.g., verifying identity, date, place, role).  
   - For partially supported claims, queries should focus on the missing or ambiguous elements.

3. **Include Domain-Specific Keywords and Phrasing:**  
   - For persons: include birthdates, roles, biographical data, relationships (e.g., “son of”, “partner of”), filmography, discography, awards, or membership affiliations.  
   - For creative works (films, games, albums): include titles, release years, director, screenwriter, cast, involved companies, production/filming locations, associated entities, and versions.  
   - For organizations, places, events: include founding dates, locations, official designations, or relevant subsets (like neighborhoods, educational districts).  
   - For events or competitions (sports, awards): include participants, results, dates, titles held, role specifics, and contextual definitions where applicable.

4. **Use Precise Search Operators and Structures if Helpful:**  
   - Use quotation marks for exact phrases.  
   - Use logical operators like AND, OR to capture variant wordings or synonyms.  
   - Add site or domain restrictions (e.g., site:imdb.com, site:discogs.com) when these improve precision.  
   - Include alternate possible names, translations, or disambiguations if relevant.

5. **Clarify Ambiguities or Request Clarifications When Needed:**  
   In cases where the claim is complex, vague, or conflates multiple facts without clear references (e.g., unclear “Netflix nomination” meaning, or ambiguous actor-role relationships), formulate queries that either seek clarification or explicitly request precise definitions or authoritative sources clarifying these ambiguities.

6. **Prioritize Authoritative and Domain-Appropriate Sources:**  
   Suggest sources like Wikipedia, IMDb, ATP/ITF, official organizational websites, census bureaus, reputable news or industry publications, database aggregators (e.g., Discogs for music, filmreference.com for film professionals), or festival archives as relevant to the claim domain.

7. **Ask for Source Citations or Direct Evidence If Possible:**  
   Where verifying complex claims, queries should aim for sources that offer direct proof, such as official credits, biographies, award announcements, or historical records.

---

**Summary:**  
From the claim and the summary evaluation of evidence availability, generate comprehensive, carefully structured, keyword-focused search queries tailored to retrieve the full scope of factual data needed to conclusively verify or refute the claim. The queries should cover each claim sub-part explicitly, leverage domain-specific terminology and relationships, and guide retrieval toward authoritative sources. If ambiguity exists, formulate queries that prompt clarifications or additional details needed for precise validation.
